<div id="review_3918091" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3918091" />
    <a title="Kay" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/241691-kay"><img alt="Kay" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1541802339p2/241691.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3918091?book_show_action=true">Aug 01, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kay" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kay" href="/user/show/241691-kay">Kay</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780061059070" href="/book/show/537050.The_Last_Continent">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/241691-kay?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/241691-kay?shelf=fantasy_sci-fi">fantasy_sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3918091" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4928713621389931862"><i>My </i>Washington Post<i> "Book World" review from May 1999</i><br/><br/><b>You Call That a Knife?</b><br/><br/>British author Terry Pratchett is sometimes referred to as "prolific," a term used almost reproachfully. Not only is he prolific, but he also writes books which for lack of a better description are classed as fantasy. "Surely," the Serious Reader sniffs, "you're joking."<br/><br/>No, mate, I'm not. Welcome to the 22nd novel in Pratchett's Discworld series, <i>The Last Continent</i>. A disclaimer on the book's jacket says that the continent</span>
  <span id="freeText4928713621389931862" style="display:none"><i>My </i>Washington Post<i> "Book World" review from May 1999</i><br /><br /><b>You Call That a Knife?</b><br /><br />British author Terry Pratchett is sometimes referred to as "prolific," a term used almost reproachfully. Not only is he prolific, but he also writes books which for lack of a better description are classed as fantasy. "Surely," the Serious Reader sniffs, "you're joking."<br /><br />No, mate, I'm not. Welcome to the 22nd novel in Pratchett's Discworld series, <i>The Last Continent</i>. A disclaimer on the book's jacket says that the continent in the title, Fourecks (XXXX) is "Not Australia. Honest." Right, pull the other one, it's got bells on. <br /><br />Pratchett, clearly an admirer of all things Australian, unleashes his perennial anti-hero, the inept wizard Rincewind, on a remarkably similar continent. His arrival has somehow altered events in the past, and now, at the insistence of a magically morphing kangaroo, he must go on a quest to bring the "Wet" back to the parched continent. Oh, and along the way he needs to put other things right by becoming the archetypal Hero of Aussie legend and song: "Champion shearer, road warrior, bush ranger, sheep-stealer, horse rider. . . "<br /><br />Rincewind will have none of this, and reacts in the way that always serves him best: He runs away. In his flight from enforced heroism, he encounters a dwarf charioteer named Mad, a horse named Snowy, a barman named Crocodile, Petunia the Desert Princess, and . . . oh, you get the picture. Just about every icon from popular Australian culture is trotted out, but it's done with such freshness and geniality that only a real stick-in-the-mud would object.<br /><br />Pratchett has a keen ear for linguistic absurdity, and he gets great mileage from Australian slang and place names. After leaving the dusty little town of "Dijabringabeeralong" on the way to "Bugarup," Rincewind encounters a group of Ecksians who competitively dredge up one colorful expression after another, reciting a litany of hilarious made-up Aussie expressions: "Isn't that `snagged as a wombat's tonker'?" "No, no, no, that's when you chuck a twister, isn't it? "<br /><br />While it's not necessary for the American reader to get all the Australian references, it helps to know, for example, the story behind the unofficial national anthem, "Waltzing Matilda," and who Banjo Paterson was. <br /><br />But many of the scenes, such as the one when a giant unsheathes a huge knife and says, "You call that a knife? This is what I call a knife!" will be instantly familiar. What Pratchett seems to be doing, frequently, is commenting on the essential absurdity of life. He places his characters, who behave in a very 20th-century, everyday way, in the unlikeliest situations, juxtaposing the probable and improbable to provide a view from a new, usually humorous perspective. <br /><br />Truth, Pratchett seems to be saying, is often stranger than fiction. Rincewind is aghast, for example, when he's served a meat pie covered in green pea puree and then topped with tomato sauce. But of course Pie Floaters are an authentic Australian dish.<br /><br />A second plot line in <i>The Last Continent</i> involves the escapades of certain members of the Unseen University: the Bursar, the Dean, the Senior Wrangler, Mrs. Whitlow, Ponder Stibbons, and the Archchancellor. The last is making a complete nuisance of himself, having picked up a few half-baked ideas from a book entitled "How to Dynamically Manage People for Dynamic Results in a Caring Empowering Way in Quite a Short Time Dynamically." <br /><br />While it helps to have a bit of Discworld background, the reader unfamiliar with the earlier books will still get the general drift. The Faculty behave like, well, faculty, bickering and backbiting. They set out to find Rincewind, who is on Fourecks, but in doing so end up on a mysterious island after crawling through a window in the study of the Egregious (rhymes with Regius) Professor of Cruel and Unusual Geography.<br /><br />The island the Faculty are stranded on is populated by only one of each species, though there are, for some reason, an extraordinary number of beetles about. It turns out the island and all its singular creatures are the work of the God of Evolution, only he hasn't quite worked all the details out yet. Furthermore, he seems to have a credibility problem. "Begone from This Place Or I Will Smite Thee!" he declares, but the Faculty are completely unimpressed. In fact, it's up to Ponder Stibbons to explain the theory of evolution to him, while Mrs. Whitlow is the only one uninhibited enough to take him aside and explain the improbable mechanics of sexual reproduction to the incredulous God.<br /><br />There are very clever, often laugh-out-loud-funny references to everything from Richard Dawkins's <i>The Selfish Gene</i> to the Butterfly Effect. At times the reader feels a game of literary "Pictionary" is taking place. Here's the God of Evolution, and he's tinkering with, what, the bill of a bird? Could it be . . . ? Yes! The Beak of the Finch! Behind all the wordplay and sly gags, though, lie philosophical questions: Why are common sense and analytical thinking so often apparently at odds, and what happens when a premise is carried to its logical extreme?<br /><br />Another theme that runs throughout the book is the serendipity of invention and the pervasiveness of the Law of Unintended Consequences. As he stumbles around Fourecks, Rincewind winds up inventing a dessert called Peach Nellie, thongs, a distinctive type of hat, and Vegemite. Meanwhile, the Faculty, by committee, create the duck-billed platypus. It's like a fantasy version of James Burke's "Connections."<br /><br />Although the two separate plot lines are maintained with amusing contrasts, when the time comes to bring the two plots together, the wizards and Rincewind meet and resolve their problems rather abruptly. The impression created is similar to that disappointing announcement of game show hosts everywhere: "Oh, but I see our time is up!" In a mere 20 pages or so all the loose ends are neatly tied together, and then the Wizards sail off into the sunset.<br /><br />Suffice it to say that while this may not be Pratchett's best Discworld novel, it's still an enjoyable one. What, one wonders, will he tackle next? Among other things, he's spoofed religion (<i>Small Gods</i>), feminism (<i>Equal Rites</i>), Hollywood (<i>Moving Pictures</i>), death (in an entire series of books), opera (<i>Maskerade</i>), racism (<i>Jingo</i>), Christmas (<i>Hogfather</i>), ancient Egypt (<i>Pyramids</i>), and most of the hoary, shop-worn devices of fantasy. Still, no worries; there are plenty of themes left.<br /><br />It would be nice, however, if Pratchett's books were released sooner in the United States.<i> The Last Continent</i> made its debut in Britain in May 1998 but didn't appear in the United States until this year. Meantime, Pratchett's 23rd Discworld book,<i> Carpe Jugulum</i>, appeared in Britain in November 1998 but has yet to make it to the States. Not only that, but Pratchett fans in search of early Discworld novels will have to order the books from the U.K. Odd that the God of Marketing hasn't rushed to fill that particular, um, niche.<br /><br />------------------<br /><br />Note: This review was subsequently included in an anthology, <i> Contemporary Literary Criticism</i>. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4928713621389931862" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3055229270" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8482864066656311222">Archchancellor Ridcully, the Dean, Burser, Senior Wrangler, Lecturer in Recent Runes, Chair of Indefinite Studies, Ponder Stibbins and the Librarian, along with Head Housekeeper, Mrs. Whitlow, who ends up with them by mistake, all find a secret room in Unseen University and are all transported back to the beginning of time and the birth of a new continent, where they meet a couple of gods. It’s not easy for the Librarian, who’s caught a bug and every time he sneezes turns into something with red</span>
  <span id="freeText8482864066656311222" style="display:none">Archchancellor Ridcully, the Dean, Burser, Senior Wrangler, Lecturer in Recent Runes, Chair of Indefinite Studies, Ponder Stibbins and the Librarian, along with Head Housekeeper, Mrs. Whitlow, who ends up with them by mistake, all find a secret room in Unseen University and are all transported back to the beginning of time and the birth of a new continent, where they meet a couple of gods. It’s not easy for the Librarian, who’s caught a bug and every time he sneezes turns into something with red fur, like a lawn chair. Those wizards get to see evolution in action. Meanwhile, Rincewind has landed on that same continent, only alone and hungry. He meets an aboriginal man who teaches him how to find grubs to eat, which he does for a few weeks. Then he meets a talking kangaroo named Scrappy who sends him on the road. He meets some drag queens, who have Luggage, who they call Trunkie. He's not sure if he's glad to have Luggage back, but he is happy for a change of underwear. He visits the towns of Dijabringabeeralong and Bugarup. This place is nothing like the Aurient. It’s all red sand, heat and no rain. No one there even knows what rain is, not even the XXXX wizards he meets, but by the time Rincewind’s been there awhile, they find out. Death visits Rincewind a couple of time, but just as a joke. As usual with these books, this one made me laugh out loud. </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer3298451909" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer7046500796744595986">Reread 2020: I'm reading this after taking a brief career break from Australia to help during the pandemic in the UK so this is full of memories of Oz for me while in my home country. I love the research Terry has put into the Indigenous Australian art and history for this book, that I clearly missed the first time reading this book as an ignorant, untravelled teenager. The in jokes about drop bears and Crocodile Dundee are also hilarious. I have definitely enjoyed this book more after living in</span>
  <span id="freeText7046500796744595986" style="display:none">Reread 2020: I'm reading this after taking a brief career break from Australia to help during the pandemic in the UK so this is full of memories of Oz for me while in my home country. I love the research Terry has put into the Indigenous Australian art and history for this book, that I clearly missed the first time reading this book as an ignorant, untravelled teenager. The in jokes about drop bears and Crocodile Dundee are also hilarious. I have definitely enjoyed this book more after living in Fourecks!</span>

<div id="review_1260017230" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1260017230" />
    <a title="Tanya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15698555-tanya"><img alt="Tanya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602837639p2/15698555.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1260017230?book_show_action=true">Dec 27, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tanya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tanya" href="/user/show/15698555-tanya">Tanya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780857524140" href="/book/show/29429529-the-last-continent">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1260017230" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12213120943914021075"><b>Who is this hero striding across the desert?<br/>Sheep shearer, beer drinker, bush ranger and someone who'll even eat a Meat Pie Floater when he's sober...</b><br/><br/>While there is no such thing as a <i>bad</i> Discworld novel, some are definitely much better than others... and falling somewhere between 2 and 3 stars for me, this witty parody of the Disc's equivalent of Australia mostly falls in the latter category.<br/><br/>Mind you, there are some awesome and unexpected references that go beyond the obvious and sort of tired </span>
  <span id="freeText12213120943914021075" style="display:none"><b>Who is this hero striding across the desert?<br />Sheep shearer, beer drinker, bush ranger and someone who'll even eat a Meat Pie Floater when he's sober...</b><br /><br />While there is no such thing as a <i>bad</i> Discworld novel, some are definitely much better than others... and falling somewhere between 2 and 3 stars for me, this witty parody of the Disc's equivalent of Australia mostly falls in the latter category.<br /><br />Mind you, there are some awesome and unexpected references that go beyond the obvious and sort of tired old jokes about weird and/or venomous animals not found anywhere else and vegemite, with Pratchett also covering more obscure Australian oddities such as the Pie Floater, local hoaxes (drop bears), relevant movies (such as <i>Mad Max</i> and <i>Priscilla, Queen of the Desert</i>), and, I'm sure, much else that went right over my head.<br /><br />While I didn't find Rincewind as insufferable as in earlier books (I did quite enjoy the previous one, <i>Interesting Times</i>, as well), it's probably because he once again didn't take center-stage, ceding a good chunk of the narrative to the Unseen University senior wizards instead—and they are a riot. So Rincewind wasn't the issue this time: My main problem was the lack of plot. Or maybe there was <i>too much</i> of it? Frankly, it was a bit of a mess, all over the place, and seemed like an afterthought, as if it was all made up just to work the Australia references and jokes into a story.<br /><br />There were more sexual innuendos than in any previous Discworld book thus far, and much more obvious ones, too; my favorite scene may have been when the wizards come across the god of evolution, who spends his time on a solitary island re-designing living things, carefully manufacturing a single copy of each, but who hasn't quite figured out how to get them to reproduce by themselves... cue embarrassed wizards attempting to explain sex to a deity who hasn't yet come up with the concept.<br /><br /><blockquote><i>'The purpose of the whole business, you see, is in fact to</i> be<i> the whole business.' (...)<br />'But surely the purpose of—I mean, wouldn't it be nice if you ended up with some creature that started to</i> think <i> about the universe—?'<br />'Good gravy, I don't want anything poking around!' said the god testily. 'There's enough patches and stitches in it as it is without some clever devil trying to find more, I can assure you. (...) Intelligence is like legs—too many and you trip yourself up.'</i></blockquote><br /><br /><i>The Last Continent</i> has its moments, but I wouldn't call it an essential book in the Discworld library; it's good fun, but it lacks a coherent plot and ultimately doesn't have anything particularly profound to offer under the veneer of humour, which is what sets my favorite books in the series apart from the others, such as this installment. Still... no worries.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>"It is said that your life flashes before your eyes just before you die. That is true, it's called Life."</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />—————<br /><br /><i>All my book reviews can be found <a href="https://watercolorstain.wordpress.com/reviews-by-title/" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a> · Buy on <a href="https://www.bookdepository.com/Last-Continent-Terry-Pratchett/9780857524140?a_aid=watercolorstain" rel="nofollow noopener">BookDepository</a></i></span>
  <a data-text-id="12213120943914021075" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('zeGQnmuNuarZsJLTHc9SOIaWb3p8YYCFCh/pkgJHpE9dijH2IFH4OiGWXANMI/TfP0n7f9zIGA9m+8W9KrJQAg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('fHQFDCpe9tYXSYnSosgrPFlbqqXdwWQk/s6p1hvsWdzsH6RkYYK3Ru9vRwLzJI3b4IQ+oH1o/K6SKoX5MxmtkQ==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

<script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_45186556'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/45186556-the-other-bennet-sister?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">The Other Bennet Sister<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/6153122.Janice_Hadlow?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Janice Hadlow<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.15 avg rating &mdash; 4,548 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2020\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer15416965421374275393\">Mary, the bookish ugly duckling of Pride and Prejudice’s five Bennet sisters, emerges from the shadows and transforms into a desired woman with choices of her own.\n\nWhat if Mary Bennet’s life took a different path from that laid out for her in Pride <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText15416965421374275393\" style=\"display:none\">Mary, the bookish ugly duckling of Pride and Prejudice’s five Bennet sisters, emerges from the shadows and transforms into a desired woman with choices of her own.\n\nWhat if Mary Bennet’s life took a different path from that laid out for her in Pride and Prejudice? What if the frustrated intellectual of the Bennet family, the marginalized middle daughter, the plain girl who takes refuge in her books, eventually found the fulfillment enjoyed by her prettier, more confident sisters? This is the plot of The Other Bennet Sister, a debut novel with exactly the affection and authority to satisfy Austen fans.\n\nUltimately, Mary’s journey is like that taken by every Austen heroine. She learns that she can only expect joy when she has accepted who she really is. She must throw off the false expectations and wrong ideas that have combined to obscure her true nature and prevented her from what makes her happy. Only when she undergoes this evolution does she have a chance at finding fulfillment; only then does she have the clarity to recognize her partner when he presents himself—and only at that moment is she genuinely worthy of love.\n\nMary’s destiny diverges from that of her sisters. It does not involve broad acres or landed gentry. But it does include a man; and, as in all Austen novels, Mary must decide whether he is the truly the one for her. In The Other Bennet Sister, Mary is a fully rounded character—complex, conflicted, and often uncertain; but also vulnerable, supremely sympathetic, and ultimately the protagonist of an uncommonly       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/1654.Terry_Pratchett">About Terry Pratchett</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/1654.Terry_Pratchett"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1235562205p3/1654.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/1654.Terry_Pratchett">Terry Pratchett
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
35,095
followers
</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10699721487568853224">Born Terence David John Pratchett, Sir Terry Pratchett sold his first story when he was thirteen, which earned him enough money to buy a second-hand typewriter. His first novel, a humorous fantasy entitled <i>The Carpet People</i>, appeared in 1971 from the publisher Colin Smythe. <br/><br/>Terry worked for many years as a journalist and press officer, writing in his spare time and publishing a number of novels, i</span>
  <span id="freeText10699721487568853224" style="display:none">Born Terence David John Pratchett, Sir Terry Pratchett sold his first story when he was thirteen, which earned him enough money to buy a second-hand typewriter. His first novel, a humorous fantasy entitled <i>The Carpet People</i>, appeared in 1971 from the publisher Colin Smythe. <br /><br />Terry worked for many years as a journalist and press officer, writing in his spare time and publishing a number of novels, including his first Discworld novel, <i>The Color of Magic</i>, in 1983. In 1987, he turned to writing full time. <br /><br />There are over 40 books in the Discworld series, of which four are written for children. The first of these, <i>The Amazing Maurice and His Educated Rodents</i>, won the Carnegie Medal. <br /><br />A non-Discworld book, <i>Good Omens</i>, his 1990 collaboration with Neil Gaiman, has been a longtime bestseller and was reissued in hardcover by William Morrow in early 2006 (it is also available as a mass market paperback - Harper Torch, 2006 - and trade paperback - Harper Paperbacks, 2006). <br /><br />In 2008, Harper Children's published Terry's standalone non-Discworld YA novel, <i>Nation</i>. Terry published <i>Snuff</i> in October 2011. <br /><br />Regarded as one of the most significant contemporary English-language satirists, Pratchett has won numerous literary awards, was named an Officer of the British Empire (OBE) “for services to literature” in 1998, and has received honorary doctorates from the University of Warwick in 1999, the University of Portsmouth in 2001, the University of Bath in 2003, the University of Bristol in 2004, Buckinghamshire New University in 2008, the University of Dublin in 2008, Bradford University in 2009, the University of Winchester in 2009, and The Open University in 2013 for his contribution to Public Service.<br /><br />In Dec. of 2007, Pratchett disclosed that he had been diagnosed with Alzheimer's disease. On 18 Feb, 2009, he was knighted by Queen Elizabeth II.<br /><br />He was awarded the World Fantasy Life Achievement Award in 2010.<br /><br />Sir Terry Pratchett passed away on 12th March 2015.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10699721487568853224" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
